<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Textile</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Various fiber-based materials</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Fabric" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Fabric_(disambiguation)" title="Fabric (disambiguation)">Fabric (disambiguation)</a> and <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Textile_(disambiguation)" title="Textile (disambiguation)">Textile (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<p><b>Textile</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Hyponymy_and_hypernymy" title="Hyponymy and hypernymy">umbrella term</a> that includes various <a href="/wiki/Fiber" title="Fiber">fiber-based</a> materials, including fibers, <a href="/wiki/Yarn" title="Yarn">yarns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Staple_(textiles)#Filament_fiber" title="Staple (textiles)">filaments</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thread_(yarn)" title="Thread (yarn)">threads</a>, different <a href="#Fabric">fabric</a> types, etc. At first, the word "textiles" only referred to <a href="/wiki/Woven_fabric" title="Woven fabric">woven fabrics</a>. However, <a href="/wiki/Weaving" title="Weaving">weaving</a> is not the only manufacturing method, and many other methods were later developed to form textile structures based on their intended use. <a href="/wiki/Knitting" title="Knitting">Knitting</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nonwoven" title="Nonwoven">non-woven</a> are other popular types of fabric manufacturing. In the contemporary world, textiles satisfy the <a href="/wiki/Material" title="Material">material</a> needs for versatile applications, from simple daily <a href="/wiki/Clothing" title="Clothing">clothing</a> to <a href="/wiki/Bulletproof_vest" title="Bulletproof vest">bulletproof jackets</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spacesuit" title="Spacesuit">spacesuits</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Medical_gown" title="Medical gown">doctor's gowns</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Textiles are divided into two groups: Domestic purpose [consumer textiles] and <a href="/wiki/Technical_textile" title="Technical textile">technical textiles</a>.  In consumer textiles, <a href="/wiki/Aesthetics_(textile)" title="Aesthetics (textile)">aesthetics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Textile_performance#Comfort" title="Textile performance">comfort</a> are the most important factors, but in technical textiles, <a href="/wiki/Textile_performance#Properties" title="Textile performance">functional properties</a> are the priority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-:7-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-:9-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Geotextile" title="Geotextile">Geotextiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indutech" title="Indutech">industrial textiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medical_textiles" title="Medical textiles">medical textiles</a>, and many other areas are examples of technical textiles, whereas <a href="/wiki/Clothing" title="Clothing">clothing</a> and furnishings are examples of consumer textiles. Each component of a textile product, including fiber, yarn, fabric, processing, and <a href="/wiki/Finishing_(textiles)" title="Finishing (textiles)">finishing</a>, affects the final product. Components may vary among various textile products as they are selected based on <a href="/wiki/Textile_performance" title="Textile performance">fitness for purpose</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-:7-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-:8-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-:9-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fiber is the smallest component of a fabric; fibers are typically spun into yarn, and yarns are used to manufacture fabrics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-:8-9">[9]</a></sup> Fiber has a hair-like appearance and a higher length-to-width ratio. The sources of fibers may be <a href="/wiki/Natural_fiber" title="Natural fiber">natural</a>, <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_fiber" title="Synthetic fiber">synthetic</a>, or both. The techniques of <a href="/wiki/Felt" title="Felt">felting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nonwoven_fabric" title="Nonwoven fabric">bonding</a> directly transform fibers into fabric. In other cases, yarns are manipulated with different fabric manufacturing systems to produce various fabric constructions. The fibers are twisted or laid out to make a long, continuous strand of yarn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-2">[2]</a></sup> Yarns are then used to make different kinds of fabric by weaving, knitting, <a href="/wiki/Crochet" title="Crochet">crocheting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macram%C3%A9" title="Macramé">knotting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tatting" title="Tatting">tatting</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Braid" title="Braid">braiding</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-6">[6]</a></sup> After manufacturing, textile materials are processed and finished to add value, such as aesthetics, physical characteristics, and increased usefulness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-:10-13">[13]</a></sup> The manufacturing of textiles is the oldest industrial <a href="/wiki/Textile_arts" title="Textile arts">art</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dyeing" title="Dyeing">Dyeing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Textile_printing" title="Textile printing">printing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Embroidery" title="Embroidery">embroidery</a> are all different decorative arts applied to textile materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:72_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-:72-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Textile">Textile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Textile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The word 'textile' comes from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> adjective <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">textilis</i></span>, meaning 'woven', which itself stems from <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">textus</i></span>, the past participle of the verb <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">texere</i></span>, 'to weave'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-5">[5]</a></sup> Originally applied to <a href="/wiki/Woven_fabric" title="Woven fabric">woven fabrics</a>, the term "textiles" is now used to encompass a diverse range of materials, including fibers, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yarns" title="Yarns">yarns</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fabrics" title="Fabrics">fabrics</a>, as well as other related items.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fabric">Fabric</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Fabric">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A fabric is defined as any thin, flexible material made from yarn, directly from fibers, polymeric film, foam, or any combination of these techniques. Fabric has a broader application than cloth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-:17-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 207">: 207 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Fabric is synonymous with cloth, material, goods, or <a href="/wiki/Piece_goods" title="Piece goods">piece goods</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-:7-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-:6-6">[6]</a></sup> The word 'fabric' also derives from Latin, with roots in the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European language</a>. Stemming most recently from the <a href="/wiki/Middle_French" title="Middle French">Middle French</a> <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">fabrique</i></span>, or 'building, thing made', and earlier from the Latin <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">fabrica</i></span> ('workshop; an art, trade; a skillful production, structure, fabric'), the noun <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">fabrica</i></span> stems from the Latin <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">faber</i></span>, or 'artisan who works in hard materials', which itself is derived from the Proto-Indo-European <i>dhabh-</i>, meaning 'to fit together'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cloth">Cloth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Cloth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brooklyn_Museum_-_Cloth_Merchant%27s_Shop_-_Basarat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="572" data-file-width="768" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Brooklyn_Museum_-_Cloth_Merchant%27s_Shop_-_Basarat.jpg/220px-Brooklyn_Museum_-_Cloth_Merchant%27s_Shop_-_Basarat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Brooklyn_Museum_-_Cloth_Merchant%27s_Shop_-_Basarat.jpg/330px-Brooklyn_Museum_-_Cloth_Merchant%27s_Shop_-_Basarat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Brooklyn_Museum_-_Cloth_Merchant%27s_Shop_-_Basarat.jpg/440px-Brooklyn_Museum_-_Cloth_Merchant%27s_Shop_-_Basarat.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brooklyn_Museum_-_Cloth_Merchant%27s_Shop_-_Basarat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cloth merchant's shop</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Museum_of_Lincolnshire_Life,_Lincoln,_England_-_DSCF1759.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Museum_of_Lincolnshire_Life%2C_Lincoln%2C_England_-_DSCF1759.JPG/220px-Museum_of_Lincolnshire_Life%2C_Lincoln%2C_England_-_DSCF1759.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Museum_of_Lincolnshire_Life%2C_Lincoln%2C_England_-_DSCF1759.JPG/330px-Museum_of_Lincolnshire_Life%2C_Lincoln%2C_England_-_DSCF1759.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Museum_of_Lincolnshire_Life%2C_Lincoln%2C_England_-_DSCF1759.JPG/440px-Museum_of_Lincolnshire_Life%2C_Lincoln%2C_England_-_DSCF1759.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Museum_of_Lincolnshire_Life,_Lincoln,_England_-_DSCF1759.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A replica <a href="/wiki/Draper" title="Draper">draper's shop</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Lincolnshire_Life" title="Museum of Lincolnshire Life">Museum of Lincolnshire Life</a>, Lincoln, England</div></div></div>
<p>Cloth is a kind of fabric that consists of a fine, flexible network of yarns. While the term "cloth" is synonymous with fabric, not all fabrics can be defined as cloth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-:17-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 207">: 207 </span></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (June 2022)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The word 'cloth' derives from the <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <span title="Old English (ca. 450-1100)-language text"><i lang="ang">clað</i></span>, meaning a 'cloth, woven or felted material to wrap around ones body', from the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic_language" title="Proto-Germanic language">Proto-Germanic</a> <span title="Germanic languages collective text"><i lang="gem">kalithaz</i></span>, similar to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Frisian_language" title="Old Frisian language">Old Frisian</a> <span title="Old Frisian-language text"><i lang="ofs">klath</i></span>, the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Dutch" title="Middle Dutch">Middle Dutch</a> <span title="Middle Dutch (ca. 1050-1350)-language text"><i lang="dum">cleet</i></span>, the <a href="/wiki/Middle_High_German" title="Middle High German">Middle High German</a> <span title="Middle High German (ca. 1050-1500)-language text"><i lang="gmh">kleit</i></span> and the <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">kleid</i></span>, all meaning 'garment'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_clothing_and_textiles" title="History of clothing and textiles">History of clothing and textiles</a></div>
<p>Textiles themselves being too fragile to survive across millennia, the tools used for <a href="/wiki/Spinning_(textiles)" title="Spinning (textiles)">spinning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Weaving" title="Weaving">weaving</a> make up most prehistoric evidence for textile working. The earliest tool for spinning was the <a href="/wiki/Spindle_(textiles)" title="Spindle (textiles)">spindle</a> to which a <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Whorl_(textiles)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Whorl (textiles) (page does not exist)">whorl</a> was eventually added. The weight of the whorl improved the thickness and twist of spun thread. Later the <a href="/wiki/Spinning_wheel" title="Spinning wheel">spinning wheel</a> was invented. Historians are unsure where; some say China and others India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>The precursor of today's textiles includes leaves, barks, fur pelts, and felted cloths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Banton Burial Cloth, the oldest existing example of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warp_(weaving)" title="Warp (weaving)">warp</a> <a href="/wiki/Ikat" title="Ikat">ikat</a> in Southeast <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, is displayed at the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_the_Philippines" title="National Museum of the Philippines">National Museum of the Philippines</a>. The cloth was most likely made by the native Asian people of the northwest <a href="/wiki/Romblon" title="Romblon">Romblon</a>.
The first clothes, worn at least 70,000 years ago and perhaps much earlier, were probably made of animal skins and helped protect early humans from the elements. At some point, people learned to weave plant fibers into textiles.
The discovery of <a href="/wiki/Flax" title="Flax">dyed flax fibers</a> in a cave in the <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Republic of Georgia</a> dated to 34,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BCE" title="BCE">BCE</a> suggests that textile-like materials were made as early as the Paleolithic era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-:14-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>The speed and scale of textile production have been altered almost beyond recognition by industrialization and the introduction of modern <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing" title="Manufacturing">manufacturing</a> techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Textile_industry">Textile industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Textile industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Textile_industry" title="Textile industry">textile industry</a> grew out of art and craft and was kept going by <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">guilds</a>.  In the 18th and 19th centuries, during the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">industrial revolution</a>, it became increasingly mechanized. In the 20th century, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> and <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technology</a> were driving forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Naming">Naming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Naming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Calico_sample_Crum_%26_Co..jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="235" data-file-width="350" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Calico_sample_Crum_%26_Co..jpg/220px-Calico_sample_Crum_%26_Co..jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Calico_sample_Crum_%26_Co..jpg/330px-Calico_sample_Crum_%26_Co..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7f/Calico_sample_Crum_%26_Co..jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Calico_sample_Crum_%26_Co..jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sample of calico printed with a six-colour machine by Walter Crum &amp; Co., from <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Crace_Calvert" title="Frederick Crace Calvert">Frederick Crace Calvert</a>, <i>Dyeing and Calico Printing</i> (1878)</div></div></div>
<p>Most textiles were called by their generic names, their place of origin, or were put into groups based loosely on manufacturing techniques, characteristics, and on their designs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:18_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-:18-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:19_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-:19-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:20_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-:20-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:21_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-:21-29">[29]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nylon" title="Nylon">Nylon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Olefin_fiber" title="Olefin fiber">Olefin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acrylic_fiber" title="Acrylic fiber">Acrylic</a> are all generic names for some synthetic fibers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-:7-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 219">: 219 </span></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Related_terms">Related terms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Related terms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The related words "<b>fabric</b>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-12">[12]</a></sup> and "<b>cloth</b>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-4">[4]</a></sup> and "<b>material</b>" are often used in textile assembly trades (such as <a href="/wiki/Tailor" title="Tailor">tailoring</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dressmaker" title="Dressmaker">dressmaking</a>) as synonyms for <i>textile</i>. However, there are subtle differences in these terms in specialized usage. A textile is any material made of interlacing fibers, including <a href="/wiki/Carpet" title="Carpet">carpeting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geotextile" title="Geotextile">geotextiles</a>, which may not necessarily be used in the production of further goods, such as <a href="/wiki/Clothing" title="Clothing">clothing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Upholstery" title="Upholstery">upholstery</a>. A <i>fabric</i> is a material made through <a href="/wiki/Woven_fabric" title="Woven fabric">weaving</a>, <a href="/wiki/Knitted_fabric" title="Knitted fabric">knitting</a>, spreading, felting, stitching, crocheting or bonding that may be used in the production of further products, such as clothing and upholstery, thus requiring a further step of the production. <i>Cloth</i> may also be used synonymously with <i>fabric</i>, but often specifically refers to a piece of fabric that has been processed or cut.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Greige goods: Textiles that are raw and unfinished are referred to as <a href="/wiki/Greige_goods" title="Greige goods">greige goods</a>. After manufacturing, the materials are processed and finished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-:10-13">[13]</a></sup></li>
<li>Piece goods: <a href="/wiki/Piece_goods" title="Piece goods">Piece goods</a> were textile materials sold in cut pieces as specified by the buyer. Piece goods were either cut from a fabric roll or made to a specific length, also known as yard goods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Well-clothed_baby.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1989" data-file-width="1989" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Well-clothed_baby.jpg/190px-Well-clothed_baby.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Well-clothed_baby.jpg/285px-Well-clothed_baby.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Well-clothed_baby.jpg/380px-Well-clothed_baby.jpg 2x" width="190"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Well-clothed_baby.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Infant" title="Infant">baby</a> wearing many items of soft <a href="/wiki/Winter" title="Winter">winter</a> clothing: <a href="/wiki/Headband" title="Headband">headband</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cap" title="Cap">cap</a>, fur-lined <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coat_(clothing)" title="Coat (clothing)">coat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scarf" title="Scarf">scarf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sweater" title="Sweater">sweater</a></div></div></div>
<p>Textiles are various materials made from fibers and yarns. The term "textile" was originally only used to refer to woven fabrics, but today it covers a broad range of subjects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-1">[1]</a></sup> Textiles are classified at various levels, such as according to fiber origin (natural or synthetic), structure (woven, knitted, nonwoven), finish, etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:18_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-:18-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:19_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-:19-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:20_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-:20-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:21_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-:21-29">[29]</a></sup>.However, there are primarily two types of textiles: 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consumer_textiles">Consumer textiles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Consumer textiles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Textiles have an assortment of uses, the most common of which are for <a href="/wiki/Clothing" title="Clothing">clothing</a> and for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Containers" title="Containers">containers</a> such as bags and <a href="/wiki/Basket" title="Basket">baskets</a>. In the household, textiles are used in <a href="/wiki/Carpet" title="Carpet">carpeting</a>, upholstered <a href="/wiki/Furniture" title="Furniture">furnishings</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Window_shade" title="Window shade">window shades</a>, <a href="/wiki/Towel" title="Towel">towels</a>, coverings for tables, beds, and other flat surfaces, and in <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a>. Textiles are used in many traditional hand <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crafts" title="Crafts">crafts</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Sewing" title="Sewing">sewing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quilting" title="Quilting">quilting</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Embroidery" title="Embroidery">embroidery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-:7-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Technical_textiles">Technical textiles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Technical textiles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%C3%9Cbung_Northern_Coast.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2964" data-file-width="3924" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/%C3%9Cbung_Northern_Coast.jpg/220px-%C3%9Cbung_Northern_Coast.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/%C3%9Cbung_Northern_Coast.jpg/330px-%C3%9Cbung_Northern_Coast.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/%C3%9Cbung_Northern_Coast.jpg/440px-%C3%9Cbung_Northern_Coast.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%C3%9Cbung_Northern_Coast.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Technical textile is a branch of textile that focuses on the protection, safety and other functional performance attributes of textiles, unlike domestic textiles, where the primary focus is aesthetics and comfort., an EOD technician wearing a bomb suit <a href="/wiki/Bomb_suit" title="Bomb suit">Explosive Ordnance Disposal (EOD) suit</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nonwoven_geotextile_containers.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="808" data-file-width="1170" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Nonwoven_geotextile_containers.jpg/220px-Nonwoven_geotextile_containers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Nonwoven_geotextile_containers.jpg/330px-Nonwoven_geotextile_containers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Nonwoven_geotextile_containers.jpg/440px-Nonwoven_geotextile_containers.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nonwoven_geotextile_containers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nonwoven <a href="/wiki/Geotextile" title="Geotextile">geotextile</a> bags are much more robust than woven bags of the same thickness.</div></div></div>
<p>Textiles produced for industrial purposes, and designed and chosen for technical characteristics beyond their appearance, are commonly referred to as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Technical_textiles" title="Technical textiles">technical textiles</a>.</i> Technical textiles include textile structures for automotive applications, medical textiles (such as implants), <a href="/wiki/Geotextile" title="Geotextile">geotextile</a> (reinforcement of embankments), agrotextiles (textiles for <a href="/wiki/Crop_protection" title="Crop protection">crop protection</a>), protective clothing (such as clothing resistant to heat and radiation for fire fighter clothing, against molten metals for welders, stab protection, and <a href="/wiki/Bullet" title="Bullet">bullet</a> proof vests).
</p><p>In the workplace, textiles can be used in industrial and scientific processes such as filtering. Miscellaneous uses include <a href="/wiki/Flag" title="Flag">flags</a>, <a href="/wiki/Backpack" title="Backpack">backpacks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tent" title="Tent">tents</a>, <a href="/wiki/Net_(device)" title="Net (device)">nets</a>, cleaning <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/rag" title="wikt:rag">rags</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transportation" title="Transportation">transportation</a> devices such as <a href="/wiki/Balloon" title="Balloon">balloons</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kite_flying" title="Kite flying">kites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sail" title="Sail">sails</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Parachute" title="Parachute">parachutes</a>; textiles are also used to provide strengthening in <a href="/wiki/Composite_material" title="Composite material">composite materials</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Fiberglass" title="Fiberglass">fibreglass</a> and industrial <a href="/wiki/Geotextile" title="Geotextile">geotextiles</a>. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_7-6"><a href="#cite_note-:7-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-:9-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to the often highly technical and legal requirements of these products, these textiles are typically tested in order to ensure they meet stringent performance requirements. Other forms of technical textiles may be produced to experiment with their scientific qualities and to explore the possible benefits they may have in the future. Threads coated with <a href="/wiki/Zinc_oxide" title="Zinc oxide">zinc oxide</a> <a href="/wiki/Nanowire" title="Nanowire">nanowires</a>, when woven into fabric, have been shown capable of "self-powering nanosystems", using vibrations created by everyday actions like wind or body movements to generate energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Significance">Significance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Significance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Textiles are all around us. The textile is a component of basic needs like food and shelter. Textiles are everywhere in our lives, from bath towels to space suits. Textiles help humans by comforting, protecting, and extending their lives. Textiles meet our clothing needs, keeping us warm in the winter and cool in the summer. There are several applications for textiles, such as medical textiles, intelligent textiles, and automotive textiles. All of them contribute to the well-being of humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Serviceability_in_textiles">Serviceability in textiles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Serviceability in textiles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The term "serviceability" refers to a textile product's ability to meet the needs of consumers. The emphasis is on knowing the target market and matching the needs of the target market to the product's serviceability. Serviceability in textiles or Performance is the ability of textile materials to withstand various conditions, environments, and hazards. Aesthetics, durability, comfort and safety, appearance retention, care, environmental impact, and cost are the serviceability concepts employed in structuring the material. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-:0-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Pages: 1 to 12">: 1 to 12 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Components">Components</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Components">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Fibers, yarns, fabric construction, and finishes and designing [of garments] are the various components of a textile product. The selection of components varies with the intended use. Henceforth, the fibers, yarns, and fabric manufacturing systems are selected with consideration of the required performance. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-:0-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Pages: 1 to 12">: 1 to 12 </span></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_and_applications">Use and applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Use and applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Commercial textiles/ Domestic textiles
</th>
<th>End uses
</th>
<th>Technical textiles/ Industrial purpose textiles
</th>
<th>End uses
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Clothing" title="Clothing">Clothing</a>
</td>
<td>Clothing items for men, women and children. <a href="/wiki/Nightwear" title="Nightwear">nightwear</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sportswear" title="Sportswear">sportswear</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lingerie" title="Lingerie">lingerie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Undergarment" title="Undergarment">undergarments</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swimsuit" title="Swimsuit">swimsuit</a>. Accessories such as <a href="/wiki/Cap" title="Cap">caps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Umbrella" title="Umbrella">umbrella</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sock" title="Sock">socks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glove" title="Glove">gloves</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Handbag" title="Handbag">handbags</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_7-7"><a href="#cite_note-:7-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:11_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-:11-43">[43]</a></sup>
</td>
<td>Agro-textiles
</td>
<td>Agro-textiles are used in <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horticulture" title="Horticulture">horticulture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">aquaculture</a>, landscape <a href="/wiki/Gardening" title="Gardening">gardening</a> and <a href="/wiki/Forestry" title="Forestry">forestry</a>. Mainly for <a href="/wiki/Crop_protection" title="Crop protection">crop protection</a>, in crop development for instance shade nets, thermal insulation and sunscreen materials, windshield, antibird nets, covering livestock protection, suppressing weed and insect control, etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Furnishing
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Upholstery" title="Upholstery">Upholstery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Curtain" title="Curtain">curtains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drapery" title="Drapery">draperies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carpet" title="Carpet">carpets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Towel" title="Towel">towels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_7-8"><a href="#cite_note-:7-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:11_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-:11-43">[43]</a></sup>
</td>
<td>Geotextile
</td>
<td>Technical textiles which are used in <a href="/wiki/Civil_engineering" title="Civil engineering">civil engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Road" title="Road">roads</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Airfield" title="Airfield">airfields</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Railroad" title="Railroad">railroads</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embankment_(transportation)" title="Embankment (transportation)">embankments</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retaining_structure" title="Retaining structure">retaining structures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reservoir" title="Reservoir">reservoirs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canal" title="Canal">canals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dam" title="Dam">dams</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stream_bed" title="Stream bed">bank</a> protection, <a href="/wiki/Coastal_engineering" title="Coastal engineering">coastal engineering</a> and <a href="/wiki/Construction" title="Construction">construction</a> site <a href="/wiki/Silt_fence" title="Silt fence">silt fences</a>, and protection of  melting glaciers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</td></tr>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_uses">Other uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Other uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Source_of_knowledge">Source of knowledge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Source of knowledge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Artifact_(archaeology)" title="Artifact (archaeology)">Artifacts</a> unearthed in various <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_excavation" title="Archaeological excavation">archaeological excavations</a> informs us about the remains of past human life and their activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Dyed flax fibers discovered in the Republic of Georgia indicate that textile-like materials were developed during the <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic period</a>. <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">Radiocarbon dates</a> the microscopic fibers to 36,000 years ago, when modern humans migrated from Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-:14-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several textile remnants, such as the Inca Empire's textile arts remnants, which embody the Incas' aesthetics and social ideals, serve as a means for disseminating information about numerous civilizations, customs, and cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are <a href="/wiki/Textile_museum" title="Textile museum">textile museums</a> that display history related to many aspects of textiles. The Textile Museum raises public awareness and appreciation of the artistic merits and cultural significance of the world's textiles on a local, national, and international scale.  <a href="/wiki/Textile_Museum_(George_Washington_University)" title="Textile Museum (George Washington University)">Textile Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, was established in 1925.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Narrative_art">Narrative art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Narrative art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bayeux_Tapestry" title="Bayeux Tapestry">Bayeux Tapestry</a> is a rare example of secular <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_art" title="Romanesque art">Romanesque art</a>. The art work depicts the <a href="/wiki/Norman_Conquest" title="Norman Conquest">Norman Conquest</a> of England in 1066.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Decorative_art">Decorative art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Decorative art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Textiles are also used for <a href="/wiki/Decorative_arts" title="Decorative arts">decorative art</a>. <a href="/wiki/Pipili_appliqu%C3%A9_work" title="Pipili appliqué work">Appliqué work of pipili</a> is decorative art of <a href="/wiki/Odisha" title="Odisha">Odisha</a>, a state in <a href="/wiki/East_India" title="East India">eastern India</a>, used for <a href="/wiki/Umbrella" title="Umbrella">umbrellas</a>, wall hangings, lamp sheds, and bags. To make a range of decorative products, colored clothes are sewn in the shapes of animals, birds, flowers, and magnificent walls on a base cloth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Architextiles">Architextiles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Architextiles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Architextiles" title="Architextiles">Architextiles</a>, a combination of the words architecture and textile, are textile-based assemblages. Awnings are a basic type of architectural textile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shahi_Lal_Dera" title="Shahi Lal Dera"> Mughal Shahi Lal Dera Tent</a>, which was a movable palace, is an example of the architextiles of the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fiber">Fiber</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Fiber">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The smallest component of a fabric is fiber; fibers are typically spun into yarn, and yarns are used to make fabrics. Fibers are very thin and hair-like structures.  The sources of fibers may be <a href="/wiki/Natural_fiber" title="Natural fiber">natural</a>, <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_fiber" title="Synthetic fiber">synthetic</a>, or both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-:0-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-:17-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Pages: 64, 69">: 64, 69 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>Natural fibers are obtained from plants, animals and minerals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_2-7"><a href="#cite_note-:0-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 18">: 18 </span></sup> Since prehistoric times, textiles have been made from natural fibers. Natural fibers are further categorized as cellulosic, protein, and mineral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-:17-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 70">: 70 </span></sup></li></ul>
<ul><li>Synthetic or manmade fibers are manufactured with <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synthesis" title="Chemical synthesis">chemical synthesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_2-8"><a href="#cite_note-:0-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 18">: 18 </span></sup></li>
<li>Semi-synthetic: A subset of synthetic or manmade fibers is semi-synthetic fiber. Rayon is a classified as a semi-synthetic fiber, made with natural polymers.</li></ul>
<p>Monomers are the building blocks of polymers. Polymers in fibers are of two types: additive or condensation. Natural fibers, such as cotton and wool, have a condensation polymer type, whereas synthetic fibers can have either an additive or a condensation polymer type. For example, <a href="/wiki/Acrylic_fiber" title="Acrylic fiber">acrylic fiber</a> and <a href="/wiki/Olefin_fiber" title="Olefin fiber">olefin fibers</a> have additive polymers, and <a href="/wiki/Nylon" title="Nylon">nylon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polyester" title="Polyester">polyester</a> are condensation polymers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-:17-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_2">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
