<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fiber_properties">Fiber properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Fiber properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Fiber properties influence textile characteristics such as aesthetics, durability, comfort, and cost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_16-6"><a href="#cite_note-:17-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 69">: 69 </span></sup>
Fineness is one of the important characteristics of the fibers. They have a greater length-to-width ratio [100 times the diameter]. Fibers need to be strong, cohesive, and flexible. The usefulness of fibers are characterized on the basis of  certain parameters such as strength, flexibility, and length to diameter ratio, and spinnability. Natural fibers are relatively short [<a href="/wiki/Staple_(textiles)" title="Staple (textiles)">staple</a>] in length. Synthetic fibers are produced in longer lengths called filaments. Silk is the only natural fiber that is a filament. The classification of fibers is based on their origin, derivation, and generic types.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_2-9"><a href="#cite_note-:0-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_16-7"><a href="#cite_note-:17-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Pages: 64, 69">: 64, 69 </span></sup>
</p><p>Certain properties of synthetic fibers, such as their diameter, <a href="/wiki/Cross_section_(fiber)" title="Cross section (fiber)">cross section</a>, and color, can be altered during production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_16-8"><a href="#cite_note-:17-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 66">: 66 </span></sup>
</p><p>Cotton: Cotton has a long history of use in the clothing due to its favorable properties. This fiber is soft, moisture-absorbent, breathable, and is renowned for its long durability.
</p>
<h4><span id="Blends_.28blended_textiles.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Blends_(blended_textiles)">Blends (blended textiles)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Blends (blended textiles)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Fabric or <a href="/wiki/Yarn" title="Yarn">yarn</a> produced with a <a href="/wiki/Combination" title="Combination">combination</a> of two or more types of different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fibre" title="Fibre">fibers</a>, or yarns to obtain desired traits. Blending is possible at various stages of <a href="/wiki/Textile_manufacturing" title="Textile manufacturing">textile manufacturing</a>. Final composition is liable for the properties of the resultant product. <a href="/wiki/Natural_fiber" title="Natural fiber">Natural</a> and <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_fiber" title="Synthetic fiber">synthetic fibers</a> are blended to overcome disadvantage of single fiber properties and to achieve better performance characteristics and aesthetic effects such as <a href="/wiki/Devor%C3%A9" title="Devoré">devoré</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heather_(fabric)" title="Heather (fabric)">heather effect, cross dyeing and stripes pattern</a> etc. <a href="/wiki/Clothing" title="Clothing">Clothing</a> woven from a blend of <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polyester" title="Polyester">polyester</a> can be more durable and easier to maintain than material woven solely from cotton. Other than sharing functional properties, blending makes the products more economical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Union or Union fabrics is the 19th century term for blended fabrics. While it is no longer in use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> <i>Mixture</i> or <i>mixed cloth</i> is another term used for blended cloths when different types of yarns are used in warp and weft sides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Blended textiles are not new.
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Mashru" title="Mashru">Mashru</a> was a 16th-century fabric, is one of the earliest forms of "mixed cloth", a material composed of silk and cotton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Siamoise" title="Siamoise">Siamoise</a> was a 17th-century cotton and linen material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">Composition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Composition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Fiber composition<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> the fiber blend composition of mixtures of the fibers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> is an important criterion to analyze the behavior, properties such as functional aspects, and commercial classification of the merchandise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most common blend is cotton and polyester. Regular blended fabric is 65% polyester and 35% cotton. It is called a <i>reverse blend</i> if the ratio of cotton predominates—the percentage of the fibers changes with the price and required properties.
</p><p>Blending adds value to the textiles; it helps in reducing the cost (artificial fibers are less expensive than natural fibers) and adding advantage in properties of the final product.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> For instance, a small amount of spandex adds stretch to the fabrics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Wool can add warmth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses_of_different_fibers">Uses of different fibers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Uses of different fibers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_fibers">Natural fibers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Natural fibers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Plant">Plant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Plant">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Poaceae" title="Poaceae">Grass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Juncaceae" title="Juncaceae">rush</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hemp" title="Hemp">hemp</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sisal" title="Sisal">sisal</a> are all used in making <a href="/wiki/Rope" title="Rope">rope</a>. In the first two, the entire plant is used for this purpose, while in the last two, only fibers from the plant are utilized. <a href="/wiki/Coir" title="Coir">Coir</a> (<a href="/wiki/Coconut" title="Coconut">coconut</a> fiber) is used in making <a href="/wiki/Twine" title="Twine">twine</a>, and also in floormats, <a href="/wiki/Mat" title="Mat">doormats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brush" title="Brush">brushes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mattress" title="Mattress">mattresses</a>, floor tiles, and <a href="/wiki/Bag" title="Bag">sacking</a>.</li></ul>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Straw" title="Straw">Straw</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bamboo_textiles" title="Bamboo textiles">bamboo</a> are both used to make hats. Straw, a dried form of grass, is also used for stuffing, as is <a href="/wiki/Ceiba_pentandra" title="Ceiba pentandra">kapok</a>.</li></ul>
<ul><li>Fibers from <a href="/wiki/Pulpwood" title="Pulpwood">pulpwood</a> trees, cotton, <a href="/wiki/Rice_paper" title="Rice paper">rice</a>, hemp, and <a href="/wiki/Urtica_dioica" title="Urtica dioica">nettle</a> are used in making <a href="/wiki/Paper" title="Paper">paper</a>.</li></ul>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">Cotton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Linen" title="Linen">flax</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jute" title="Jute">jute</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hemp" title="Hemp">hemp</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modal_(textile)" title="Modal (textile)">modal</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banana_fiber" title="Banana fiber">banana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bamboo_textile" title="Bamboo textile">bamboo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lotus_silk" title="Lotus silk">lotus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eucalyptus" title="Eucalyptus">eucalyptus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morus_(plant)" title="Morus (plant)">mulberry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a> are all used in clothing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pi%C3%B1a" title="Piña">Piña</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pineapple" title="Pineapple">pineapple</a> fiber) and <a href="/wiki/Ramie" title="Ramie">ramie</a> are also fibers used in clothing, generally with a blend of other fibers such as cotton. Nettles have also been used to make a fiber and fabric very similar to hemp or flax. The use of milkweed stalk fiber has also been reported, but it tends to be somewhat weaker than other fibers like hemp or flax.</li></ul>
<ul><li>The inner bark of the <a href="/wiki/Lagetta_lagetto" title="Lagetta lagetto">lacebark tree</a> is a fine netting that has been used to make clothing and accessories as well as utilitarian articles such as rope.</li></ul>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Cellulose_acetate" title="Cellulose acetate">Acetate</a> is used to increase the shininess of certain fabrics such as <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Velvet" title="Velvet">velvets</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Taffeta" title="Taffeta">taffetas</a>.</li></ul>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Seaweed" title="Seaweed">Seaweed</a> is used in the production of textiles: a water-soluble fiber known as <a href="/wiki/Alginic_acid" title="Alginic acid">alginate</a> is produced and is used as a holding fiber; when the cloth is finished, the alginate is dissolved, leaving an open area.</li></ul>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Rayon" title="Rayon">Rayon</a> is a manufactured fabric derived from plant pulp. Different types of rayon can imitate <a href="/wiki/Hand_feel" title="Hand feel">feel</a> and texture of silk, cotton, wool, or linen.</li></ul>
<p>Fibers from the stalks of plants, such as hemp, flax, and nettles, are also known as 'bast' fibers. Hemp Fiber is yellowish-brown fiber made from the hemp plant. The fiber characteristics are coarser, harsher, strong and lightweight. Hemp fiber is used primary to make twine, rope and cordage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal">Animal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Animal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Animal textiles are commonly made from <a href="/wiki/Hair" title="Hair">hair</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fur" title="Fur">fur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skin" title="Skin">skin</a> or <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a> (in the case of silkworms).
</p>
<ul><li>Wool refers to the hair of the domestic <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">sheep</a> or <a href="/wiki/Goat" title="Goat">goat</a>, which is distinguished from other types of animal hair in that the individual strands are coated with scales and tightly crimped, and the wool as a whole is coated with a <a href="/wiki/Wax" title="Wax">wax</a> mixture known as <a href="/wiki/Lanolin" title="Lanolin">lanolin</a> (sometimes called wool grease), which is waterproof and dirtproof.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> The lanolin and other contaminants are removed from the raw wool before further processing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woollen" title="Woollen">Woolen</a> refers to a yarn produced from carded, non-parallel fibre, while <a href="/wiki/Worsted" title="Worsted">worsted</a> refers to a finer yarn spun from longer fibers which have been combed to be parallel.
<ul><li>Other animal textiles which are made from hair or fur are <i>alpaca wool</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Vicu%C3%B1a" title="Vicuña">vicuña wool</a></i>, <i>llama wool</i>, and <i>camel hair</i>, generally used in the production of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coat_(clothing)" title="Coat (clothing)">coats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jacket" title="Jacket">jackets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poncho" title="Poncho">ponchos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blanket" title="Blanket">blankets</a>, and other warm coverings.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Cashmere_wool" title="Cashmere wool">Cashmere</a></i>, the hair of the Indian <a href="/wiki/Cashmere_goat" title="Cashmere goat">cashmere goat</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mohair" title="Mohair">mohair</a>, the hair of the North African <a href="/wiki/Angora_goat" title="Angora goat">angora goat</a>, are types of wool known for their softness. Used in the production of sweaters and scarfs.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Angora_wool" title="Angora wool">Angora</a></i> refers to the long, thick, soft hair of the <a href="/wiki/Angora_rabbit" title="Angora rabbit">angora rabbit</a>. <a href="/wiki/Qiviut" title="Qiviut">Qiviut</a> is the fine inner wool of the <a href="/wiki/Muskox" title="Muskox">muskox</a>.</li></ul></li>
<li>Silk is an animal textile made from the fibres of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cocoon_(silk)" title="Cocoon (silk)">cocoon</a> of the Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silkworm" title="Silkworm">silkworm</a> which is spun into a smooth fabric prized for its softness. There are two main types of the silk: 'mulberry silk' produced by the <a href="/wiki/Bombyx_mori" title="Bombyx mori">Bombyx Mori</a>, and 'wild silk' such as <a href="/wiki/Wild_silk" title="Wild silk">Tussah silk</a> (wild silk). Silkworm larvae produce the first type if cultivated in habitats with fresh mulberry leaves for consumption, while Tussah silk is produced by silkworms feeding purely on oak leaves. Around four-fifths of the world's silk production consists of cultivated silk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Silk production consists of pillow covers, dresses, tops, skirts, bed sheets, curtains.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Microbes">Microbes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Microbes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bacterial_cellulose" title="Bacterial cellulose">Bacterial cellulose</a> can be made from industrial organic and agricultural waste, and used as material for textiles and clothing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mineral">Mineral</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Mineral">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>Asbestos and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basalt_fibre" title="Basalt fibre">basalt fibre</a> are used for vinyl tiles, sheeting and adhesives, "transite" panels and siding, acoustical ceilings, stage curtains, and fire blankets.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glass_fibre" title="Glass fibre">Glass fibre</a> is used in the production of ironing board and mattress covers, ropes and cables, reinforcement fibre for composite materials, insect netting, flame-retardant and protective fabric, soundproof, fireproof, and insulating fibres. Glass fibres are woven and coated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teflon" title="Teflon">Teflon</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Beta_cloth" title="Beta cloth">beta cloth</a>, a virtually fireproof fabric which replaced nylon in the outer layer of United States <a href="/wiki/Space_suit" title="Space suit">space suits</a> since 1968.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (January 2018)">verification needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>Metal fibre, metal foil, and metal wire have a variety of uses, including the production of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cloth-of-gold" title="Cloth-of-gold">cloth-of-gold</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jewellery" title="Jewellery">jewellery</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hardware_cloth" title="Hardware cloth">Hardware cloth</a> (US term only) is a coarse woven <a href="/wiki/Mesh" title="Mesh">mesh</a> of steel wire, used in construction. It is much like standard <a href="/wiki/Window_screen" title="Window screen">window screening</a>, but heavier and with a more open weave.</li></ul>
<p>Minerals and natural and synthetic fabrics may be combined, as in <a href="/wiki/Emery_cloth" title="Emery cloth">emery cloth</a>, a layer of <a href="/wiki/Emery_(rock)" title="Emery (rock)">emery</a> abrasive glued to a cloth backing. Also, "sand cloth" is a U.S. term for fine wire mesh with abrasive glued to it, employed like emery cloth or coarse <a href="/wiki/Sandpaper" title="Sandpaper">sandpaper</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Synthetic">Synthetic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Synthetic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the 20th century, they were supplemented by artificial fibers made from <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>. Textiles are made in various strengths and degrees of durability, from the finest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microfibre" title="Microfibre">microfibre</a> made of strands thinner than one <a href="/wiki/Units_of_textile_measurement#Denier" title="Units of textile measurement">denier</a> to the sturdiest <a href="/wiki/Canvas" title="Canvas">canvas</a>. 
</p><p>Synthetic textiles are used primarily in the production of clothing, as well as the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Geotextile" title="Geotextile">geotextiles</a>. Synthetic fibers are those that are chemically constructed, therefore are unsustainable. 
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Polyester" title="Polyester">Polyester</a> fibre is used in all types of clothing, either alone or blended with fibres such as cotton.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aramid" title="Aramid">Aramid</a> fibre (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Twaron" title="Twaron">Twaron</a>) is used for flame-retardant clothing, cut-protection, and armour.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acrylic_fibre" title="Acrylic fibre">Acrylic</a> is a fibre used to imitate wools,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> including cashmere, and is often used in replacement of them.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nylon" title="Nylon">Nylon</a> is a fibre used to imitate silk; it is used in the production of <a href="/wiki/Pantyhose" title="Pantyhose">pantyhose</a>. Thicker nylon fibres are used in <a href="/wiki/Rope" title="Rope">rope</a> and outdoor clothing.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Spandex" title="Spandex">Spandex</a> (trade name <i>Lycra</i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Polyurethane" title="Polyurethane">polyurethane</a> product that can be made tight-fitting without impeding movement. It is used to make <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Activewear" title="Activewear">activewear</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brassiere" title="Brassiere">bras</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Swimsuit" title="Swimsuit">swimsuits</a>.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olefin_fibre" title="Olefin fibre">Olefin fibre</a> is a fibre used in activewear, linings, and warm clothing. Olefins are hydrophobic, allowing them to dry quickly. A sintered felt of olefin fibres is sold under the trade name <a href="/wiki/Tyvek" title="Tyvek">Tyvek</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ingeo" title="Ingeo">Ingeo</a> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polylactide" title="Polylactide">polylactide</a> fibre blended with other fibres such as cotton and used in clothing. It is more hydrophilic than most other synthetics, allowing it to wick away perspiration.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lurex_(yarn)" title="Lurex (yarn)">Lurex</a> is a metallic fibre used in clothing embellishment.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">Milk</a> proteins have also been used to create synthetic fabric. Milk or <a href="/wiki/Casein" title="Casein">casein</a> fibre cloth was developed during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> in Germany, and further developed in Italy and America during the 1930s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Milk fibre fabric is not very durable and wrinkles easily, but has a pH similar to human skin and possesses anti-bacterial properties. It is marketed as a <a href="/wiki/Biodegradation" title="Biodegradation">biodegradable</a>, <a href="/wiki/Renewable_resource" title="Renewable resource">renewable</a> synthetic fibre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_fibre" title="Carbon fibre">Carbon fibre</a> is mostly used in composite materials, together with resin, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_fibre_reinforced_plastic" title="Carbon fibre reinforced plastic">carbon fibre reinforced plastic</a>. The fibres are made from polymer fibres through carbonization.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_methods">Production methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Production methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Textile_manufacturing" title="Textile manufacturing">Textile manufacturing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Textile_industry" title="Textile industry">Textile industry</a></div>
<p>Textile manufacturing has progressed from prehistoric crafts to a fully automated industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_16-9"><a href="#cite_note-:17-16">[16]</a></sup> Over the years, there have been continuous improvements in fabric structure and design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-:12-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Production methods
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Production method
</th>
<th>Description
</th>
<th>Inventors, inventions and milestones in progression
</th>
<th>
</th></tr>
<tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<td><a href="/wiki/Barkcloth" title="Barkcloth">Barkcloth</a>
</td>
<td>Barkcloth is made by pounding bark until it is soft and flat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_2-10"><a href="#cite_note-:0-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 245">: 245 </span></sup>
</td>
<td>Bark is an older known fabric; ancient people around the world wore bark cloth daily until woven fabrics replaced it. In <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, the bark cloth is associated with the <a href="/wiki/Torajan_people" title="Torajan people">Torajan people</a>, who made it from the fermentation of inner bark of certain local trees, mulberry and pandanus. The Torajans used stones and wooden beaters to produce bark cloth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</td>
<td><div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Barkcloth_dress_of_Lore_Bada,_Central_Sulawesi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4032" data-file-width="3024" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Barkcloth_dress_of_Lore_Bada%2C_Central_Sulawesi.jpg/220px-Barkcloth_dress_of_Lore_Bada%2C_Central_Sulawesi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Barkcloth_dress_of_Lore_Bada%2C_Central_Sulawesi.jpg/330px-Barkcloth_dress_of_Lore_Bada%2C_Central_Sulawesi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Barkcloth_dress_of_Lore_Bada%2C_Central_Sulawesi.jpg/440px-Barkcloth_dress_of_Lore_Bada%2C_Central_Sulawesi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Barkcloth_dress_of_Lore_Bada,_Central_Sulawesi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Barkcloth dress of Lore Bada people in Lore Valley, Poso Regency, Central Sulawesi, Indonesia. This collection of Central Sulawesi Museum was exhibited in Textile Museum Jakarta in November 2016.</div></div></div></div>
</td></tr>
<tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<td><a href="/wiki/Felt" title="Felt">Felting</a>
</td>
<td>Felting is a method of manufacturing fabric directly from fibers by entangling, interlocking the fibers by rubbing and pressing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_2-11"><a href="#cite_note-:0-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 245">: 245 </span></sup>
</td>
<td>Both felt and <a href="/wiki/Tapa_cloth" title="Tapa cloth">Tapa cloth</a> (a type of bark cloth) have a history that dates back to antiquity. Felting is another old method of fabric manufacturing. While civilizations in the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">western Mediterranean</a>  improved their weaving skills, <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomads</a> in <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a> learned how to make felt, a non-woven material, from wool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_16-10"><a href="#cite_note-:17-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 5">: 5 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_2-12"><a href="#cite_note-:0-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 245">: 245 </span></sup>Felting involves applying pressure and friction to a mat of fibers, working and rubbing them together until the fibers become interlocked and tangled, forming a nonwoven textile. A liquid, such as soapy water, is usually added to lubricate the fibers, and to open up the microscopic scales on strands of wool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_2-13"><a href="#cite_note-:0-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 245">: 245 </span></sup>
</td>
<td><div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kazah_jurta.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Kazah_jurta.jpg/220px-Kazah_jurta.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Kazah_jurta.jpg/330px-Kazah_jurta.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Kazah_jurta.jpg/440px-Kazah_jurta.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kazah_jurta.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kazakhs" title="Kazakhs">Kazakh</a> felt <a href="/wiki/Yurt" title="Yurt">yurt</a></div></div></div></div>
</td></tr>
<tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<td><a href="/wiki/Nonwoven_fabric" title="Nonwoven fabric">Nonwoven</a>
</td>
<td>Nonwoven fabrics are manufactured by the bonding of fibers to make fabric. Bonding may be thermal, mechanical, or chemical, or adhesives can be used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</td>
<td>Nonwovens and felting share a similar history. The first fabric that was made without yarn [nonwoven] was felt. Later on, needle punched, bonded nonwovens were developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_16-11"><a href="#cite_note-:17-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 5">: 5 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_2-14"><a href="#cite_note-:0-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 245">: 245 </span></sup>
</td>
<td><div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Material_Non-Woven_Fabric.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1309" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Material_Non-Woven_Fabric.jpg/220px-Material_Non-Woven_Fabric.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Material_Non-Woven_Fabric.jpg/330px-Material_Non-Woven_Fabric.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Material_Non-Woven_Fabric.jpg/440px-Material_Non-Woven_Fabric.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Material_Non-Woven_Fabric.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nonwoven fabrics</div></div></div></div>
</td></tr>
<tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<td><a href="/wiki/Weaving" title="Weaving">Weaving</a>
</td>
<td>Weaving is a textile production method which involves interlacing a set of longer <a href="/wiki/Yarn" title="Yarn">threads</a> (called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warp_(weaving)" title="Warp (weaving)">warp</a>) with a set of crossing threads (called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weft" title="Weft">weft</a>). This is done on a frame or machine known as a loom, of which there are a number of types. Some weaving is still done by hand, but the vast majority is mechanized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-:12-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Pages: 1–14">: 1–14 </span></sup>
</td>
<td>Handlooms: Early <a href="/wiki/Loom" title="Loom">Looms</a> date to 5000 BC. From antiquity until the mediaeval times, the loom improved in both Asia and Europe, despite the fact that the loom's fundamental operation remained unchanged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> In 200 BC, the Chinese invented vertical looms and pedal looms, transforming the craft into an industry. By decreasing the worker's workload, innovative solutions improved productivity.
<p>There were harnesses and <a href="/wiki/Heddle" title="Heddle">heddles</a> to govern the movement of the warp yarn, a shuttle to transport the weft yarns, a reed to compact the cloth, and a take up roller to roll down the cloth. By the 1st century AD, all necessary components for a loom were assembled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_16-12"><a href="#cite_note-:17-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 6">: 6 </span></sup>
</p><p>Power looms: <a href="/wiki/John_Kay_(flying_shuttle)" title="John Kay (flying shuttle)">John Kay</a> invented the <a href="/wiki/Flying_shuttle" title="Flying shuttle">flying shuttle</a> in 1734 in Bury, Lancashire. It was one of the first innovations in the cotton woven fabric industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Crompton" title="Samuel Crompton">Samuel Crompton</a> invented a <a href="/wiki/Spinning_mule" title="Spinning mule">spinning machine</a>  in 1779 that produced yarn faster than ever before. Then <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Cartwright" title="Edmund Cartwright">Edmund Cartwright</a> invented the first <a href="/wiki/Power_loom" title="Power loom">power loom</a> in 1785.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_16-13"><a href="#cite_note-:17-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 10">: 10 </span></sup>
</p><p>Jacquard loom: The <a href="/wiki/Jacquard_machine" title="Jacquard machine">Jacquard machine</a> was a modified version of programmable loom developed in 1804. It was developed by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Marie_Jacquard" title="Joseph Marie Jacquard">Joseph Marie Jacquard</a> based on earlier inventions by <a href="/wiki/Basile_Bouchon" title="Basile Bouchon">Basile Bouchon</a> (1725), Jean Baptiste Falcon (1728), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jacques_Vaucanson" title="Jacques Vaucanson">Jacques Vaucanson</a> (1740).
</p><p>The industrial revolution in the 18th century led to mass production of yarn and cloth, which led to the growth of the woven fabric part of the textile industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
</td>
<td><div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Warp_and_weft_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="418" data-file-width="377" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Warp_and_weft_2.jpg/220px-Warp_and_weft_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Warp_and_weft_2.jpg/330px-Warp_and_weft_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5e/Warp_and_weft_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Warp_and_weft_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Warp and weft</div></div></div></div>
</td></tr>
<tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<td><a href="/wiki/Knitting" title="Knitting">Knitting</a>
</td>
<td>Knitting involve interlacing loops of yarn, which are formed either on a <a href="/wiki/Knitting_needle" title="Knitting needle">knitting needle</a>, needle, or on a <a href="/wiki/Crochet_hook" title="Crochet hook">crochet hook</a>, together in a line. The processes are different in that knitting has several active loops at one time, on the knitting needle waiting to interlock with another loop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:13_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-:13-90">[90]</a></sup>
</td>
<td>Hand knitting: Though knitting was developed by <a href="/wiki/Danes" title="Danes">Danes</a> around 900 BC it did not reach to other civilizations until 900 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_16-14"><a href="#cite_note-:17-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 5">: 5 </span></sup> Europe learned to knit by hand around 1400. Three to four <a href="/wiki/Stocking" title="Stocking">stockings</a> could be knit in a week by 1450. <a href="/wiki/William_Lee_(inventor)" title="William Lee (inventor)">William Lee</a> invented a <a href="/wiki/Stocking_frame" title="Stocking frame">stocking frame</a> in 1589 that could knit one stocking per day. Acceptance of Lee's invention and subsequent modifications resulted in a wide range of fabrics in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_16-15"><a href="#cite_note-:17-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 8">: 8 </span></sup>
</td>
<td><div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Knit-schematic.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="460" data-file-width="620" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Knit-schematic.png/220px-Knit-schematic.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Knit-schematic.png/330px-Knit-schematic.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Knit-schematic.png/440px-Knit-schematic.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Knit-schematic.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Loop formation. Structure of stockinette stitch in a knitted fabric.</div></div></div></div>
</td></tr>
<tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<td>Looping, and <a href="/wiki/Crochet" title="Crochet">crocheting</a>
</td>
<td>Looping and crocheting never have more than one active loop on the needle. Knitting can be performed by machine, but crochet can only be performed by hand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:13_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-:13-90">[90]</a></sup>
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/N%C3%A5lebinding" title="Nålebinding">Nålebinding</a> is a precursor of crocheting and knitting.
<p>Crocheting is type of knitting which was  originally practised by <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scottish</a> peasants with a small, hooked needle known as a shepherd's hook.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
</td>
<td><div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crochet-round.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Crochet-round.jpg/220px-Crochet-round.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Crochet-round.jpg/330px-Crochet-round.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5f/Crochet-round.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crochet-round.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Most crochet uses one hook and works upon one stitch at a time. Crochet may be worked in circular rounds without any specialized tools, as shown here.</div></div></div></div>
</td></tr>
<tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<td><a href="/wiki/Spread_tow_fabric" title="Spread tow fabric">Spread tow</a>
</td>
<td>Spread tow is a production method where the tow fibers are spread into thin tapes, and then the tapes are woven as warp and weft. This method is mostly used for composite materials; spread tow fabrics can be made in <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fibers" title="Carbon fibers">carbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aramid" title="Aramid">aramid</a> and other fibers.
</td>
<td>
</td>
<td>
</td></tr>
<tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<td><a href="/wiki/Braid" title="Braid">Braiding</a> or plaiting
</td>
<td>Braiding or plaiting involves intertwining threads together into cloth. <a href="/wiki/Knot" title="Knot">Knotting</a> involves tying threads together and is used in making <a href="/wiki/Tatting" title="Tatting">tatting</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macrame" title="Macrame">macrame</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-:12-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 279">: 279 </span></sup>
</td>
<td>
</td>
<td><div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Braid_StepBystep.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="567" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Braid_StepBystep.jpg/220px-Braid_StepBystep.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Braid_StepBystep.jpg/330px-Braid_StepBystep.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Braid_StepBystep.jpg/440px-Braid_StepBystep.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Braid_StepBystep.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Braiding</div></div></div></div>
</td></tr>
<tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<td><a href="/wiki/Lace" title="Lace">Lace</a>
</td>
<td>Lace is made by interlocking threads together independently, using a backing alongside any of the methods described above, to create a fine fabric with open holes in the work. Lace can be made by either hand or machine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</td>
<td>
</td>
<td><div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ursuline_lace_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1712" data-file-width="2288" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Ursuline_lace_2.jpg/220px-Ursuline_lace_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Ursuline_lace_2.jpg/330px-Ursuline_lace_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Ursuline_lace_2.jpg/440px-Ursuline_lace_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ursuline_lace_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lace structure</div></div></div></div>
</td></tr>
<tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<td><a href="/wiki/3D_textiles" title="3D textiles">3D textiles</a>
</td>
<td>Fabric manufacturing by <a href="/wiki/3D_printing" title="3D printing">3D printer</a> employs additive manufacturing, also known as additive layer manufacturing (ALM), a <a href="/wiki/Computer-aided_design" title="Computer-aided design">CAD-aided</a> manufacturing technique that builds the object layer by layer. The method is used in manufacturing  of <a href="/wiki/Auxetics" title="Auxetics">Auxetic</a> textiles and in composite materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</td>
<td>
</td>
<td><div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:XYZprinting_inBloom_Dress_3D_Printed_Outfit_(16837487086).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/XYZprinting_inBloom_Dress_3D_Printed_Outfit_%2816837487086%29.jpg/220px-XYZprinting_inBloom_Dress_3D_Printed_Outfit_%2816837487086%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/XYZprinting_inBloom_Dress_3D_Printed_Outfit_%2816837487086%29.jpg/330px-XYZprinting_inBloom_Dress_3D_Printed_Outfit_%2816837487086%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/XYZprinting_inBloom_Dress_3D_Printed_Outfit_%2816837487086%29.jpg/440px-XYZprinting_inBloom_Dress_3D_Printed_Outfit_%2816837487086%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:XYZprinting_inBloom_Dress_3D_Printed_Outfit_(16837487086).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>3D-printed outfit</div></div></div></div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Important parameters in fabric selection: 
</p><p>The primary consideration in fabric selection is the end use. The fabric needs vary greatly depending on the application. Similar types of fabric may not be suitable for all applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_16-16"><a href="#cite_note-:17-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 18">: 18 </span></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Units_of_textile_measurement#Grams_per_square_metre_(GSM)" title="Units of textile measurement">Fabric weight</a> is an important criteria while producing different fabrics. A carpet requires a fabric with 1300 GSM, but a robe may be made with 160 GSM. Certainly, fabrics for clothes and carpets have distinct weights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_16-17"><a href="#cite_note-:17-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 18">: 18 </span></sup>
</p>

<p>Stretchable fabrics have greater movability and are thus more comfortable than fabrics with no stretch or less stretch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_16-18"><a href="#cite_note-:17-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 23">: 23 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Textile_exports">Textile exports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Textile exports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="wikitable floatright">

<td style="text-align:right;">274

<p>According to the United Nations Commodity Trade Statistics Database, the global textiles and apparel export market reached $772 billion in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Changing_dynamics_of_the_market">Changing dynamics of the market</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Changing dynamics of the market">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>China is the largest exporter of textile goods. The majority of China's exports consist of apparel, apparel accessories, textile yarns, and textile products. The competitive advantages of the China are low prices and abundant labor, lowered commercial obstacles, and a ready supply of raw materials. China, along with the United States and India, is a major producer of cotton. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>China's apparel market share has declined in recent years due to various reasons and a shift toward high-end, sophisticated products.  Additionally, the investors from China made stakes in Myanmar, Vietnam, and Cambodia. Last year, its market share was 36.7%, or $161 billion, a decline of 8% year-over-year. In other words, China lost $14 billion in garment work orders to other countries in a single year. In 2016, Bangladesh's apparel market share was valued at $28 billion, increasing 7.69 percent from the previous year.
</p><p>In 2016, China ($161 billion), Bangladesh ($28 billion), Vietnam ($25 billion), India ($18 billion), Hong Kong ($16 billion), Turkey ($15 billion), and Indonesia ($7 billion) were the leading exporters of apparel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Finishing">Finishing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Finishing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Old_method_of_Bleaching_Figure_20_Appleton_1884.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3939" data-file-width="2739" decoding="async" height="316" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Old_method_of_Bleaching_Figure_20_Appleton_1884.tif/lossless-page1-220px-Old_method_of_Bleaching_Figure_20_Appleton_1884.tif.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Old_method_of_Bleaching_Figure_20_Appleton_1884.tif/lossless-page1-330px-Old_method_of_Bleaching_Figure_20_Appleton_1884.tif.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Old_method_of_Bleaching_Figure_20_Appleton_1884.tif/lossless-page1-440px-Old_method_of_Bleaching_Figure_20_Appleton_1884.tif.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Old_method_of_Bleaching_Figure_20_Appleton_1884.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early method of bleaching cotton and linen goods on lawns</div></div></div>
<p>The fabric, when it leaves a loom or knitting machine, is not readily usable. It may be rough, uneven, or have flaws like skewing. Hence, it is necessary to finish the fabric. Finishing techniques enhance the value of the treated fabrics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_16-19"><a href="#cite_note-:17-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 6">: 6 </span></sup> After manufacturing, textiles undergo a range of finishing procedures, including bleaching, dyeing, printing, as well as mechanical and chemical finishing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-:10-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coloration">Coloration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Coloration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Textiles are often <a href="/wiki/Dyeing" title="Dyeing">dyed</a>, with fabrics available in almost every <a href="/wiki/Color_of_clothing" title="Color of clothing">colour</a>. The dyeing process often requires several dozen gallons of water for each pound of clothing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Coloured designs in textiles can be created by weaving together fibres of different colours (<a href="/wiki/Tartan" title="Tartan">tartan</a> or Uzbek Ikat), adding coloured stitches to finished fabric (<a href="/wiki/Embroidery" title="Embroidery">embroidery</a>), creating patterns by <a href="/wiki/Resist_dyeing" title="Resist dyeing">resist dyeing</a> methods, tying off areas of cloth and dyeing the rest (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tie-dyeing" title="Tie-dyeing">tie-dyeing</a>), drawing wax designs on cloth and dyeing in between them (<a href="/wiki/Batik" title="Batik">batik</a>), or using various printing processes on finished fabric. <a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing" title="Woodblock printing">Woodblock printing</a>, still used in India and elsewhere today, is the oldest of these dating back to at least 220 CE in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>. Textiles are also sometimes <a href="/wiki/Bleach" title="Bleach">bleached</a>, making the textile pale or white.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Finishes">Finishes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Finishes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Textile finishing is the process of converting the loomstate or raw goods into a useful product, which can be done mechanically or chemically. Finishing is a broad term that refers to a variety of physical and chemical techniques and treatments that finish one stage of textile production while also preparing for the next. Textile finishing can include aspects like improving surface feel, aesthetical enhancement, and adding advanced chemical finishes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-:10-13">[13]</a></sup> A finish is any process that transforms <a href="/wiki/Greige_goods" title="Greige goods">unfinished products</a> into finished products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> This includes mechanical finishing and chemical applications which alter the composition of treated textiles (fiber, yarn or fabric.)
</p><p>Since the 1990s, with advances in technologies such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Permanent_press" title="Permanent press">permanent press</a> process, <a href="/wiki/Finishing_(textiles)" title="Finishing (textiles)">finishing</a> agents have been used to strengthen fabrics and make them wrinkle free.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> More recently, nanomaterials research has led to additional advancements, with companies such as Nano-Tex and NanoHorizons developing permanent treatments based on metallic <a href="/wiki/Nanoparticle" title="Nanoparticle">nanoparticles</a> for making textiles more resistant to things such as water, stains, wrinkles, and pathogens such as bacteria and fungi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>Textiles receive a range of treatments before they reach the end-user. From <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a> finishes (to improve crease-resistance) to biocidic finishes and from flame retardants to dyeing of many types of fabric, the possibilities are almost endless. However, many of these finishes may also have detrimental effects on the end user. A number of disperse, acid and reactive dyes, for example, have been shown to be allergenic to sensitive individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Further to this, specific dyes within this group have also been shown to induce purpuric contact dermatitis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p><span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Eisengarn" title="Eisengarn">Eisengarn</a></i></span>, meaning "iron yarn" in English, is a light-reflecting, strong material invented in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> in the 19th century. It is made by soaking cotton threads in a starch and paraffin wax solution. The threads are then stretched and polished by steel rollers and brushes. The result of the process is a lustrous, tear-resistant yarn which is extremely hardwearing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-history-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-video_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-video-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p>

<td><a href="/wiki/Carpet" title="Carpet">Carpets</a>, rugs, velvet, <a href="/wiki/Velour" title="Velour">velour</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Velveteen" title="Velveteen">velveteen</a>, referred to as pile fabrics, are made by interlacing a secondary yarn through woven cloth, creating a tufted layer known as a <a href="/wiki/Nap_(fabric)" title="Nap (fabric)">nap</a> or <a href="/wiki/Pile_(textile)" title="Pile (textile)">pile</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_7-12"><a href="#cite_note-:7-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 196">: 196 </span></sup>

The machine operates similarly to a lawn mower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_7-13"><a href="#cite_note-:7-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 197">: 197 </span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Moleskin" title="Moleskin">Moleskin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Velvet" title="Velvet">velvet</a> are sheared materials in which pile is cut to a certain level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_and_health_impacts">Environmental and health impacts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Environmental and health impacts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although formaldehyde levels in clothing are unlikely to be at levels high enough to cause an allergic reaction,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> due to the presence of such a chemical, quality control and testing are of utmost importance. Flame retardants (mainly in the brominated form) are also of concern where the environment, and their potential toxicity, are concerned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_and_health_effects_of_textile_industry_wastewater">Environmental and health effects of textile industry wastewater</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Environmental and health effects of textile industry wastewater">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Many kind of respiratory diseases, skin problems, and allergies may be caused by dyes and pigments discharged into the water.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Testing">Testing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Testing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Testing for these additives is possible at a number of commercial laboratories.  It is also possible to have textiles tested according to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oeko-tex" title="Oeko-tex">Oeko-tex</a> certification standard, which contains limits levels for the use of certain chemicals in textiles products.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Laws_and_regulations">Laws and regulations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Laws and regulations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Different countries have certain laws and regulations to protect consumers' interests.  The <a href="/wiki/Textile_Fiber_Products_Identification_Act" title="Textile Fiber Products Identification Act">Textile Fiber Products Identification Act</a> is a law that protects consumers in the United States. The act protects producer and consumer interests by implementing labelling (required content disclosure) and advertising requirements on textile products. The Textile Fiber Products Identification Act applies to all textile fiber products besides wool, which is governed by the Wool Product Label Number. The law prohibits misinformation about the fiber content, misbranding, and any unfair advertising practice, as well as requires businesses to operate in a particular manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_7-14"><a href="#cite_note-:7-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Testing_of_textiles">Testing of textiles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Textile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Testing of textiles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Testing occurs at various stages of the textile manufacturing process, from raw material to finished product. The purpose of testing is to evaluate and analyze the regulatory compliance, the product's quality and performance, as well as to measure its specifications. Textile testing encompasses a wide range of methodologies, procedures, equipment, and sophisticated <a href="/wiki/Laboratory" title="Laboratory">laboratories</a>. Local governments and authorized organization's such as <a href="/wiki/ASTM_International" title="ASTM International">ASTM International</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Standardization" title="International Organization for Standardization">International Organization for Standardization</a>, and <a href="/wiki/American_Association_of_Textile_Chemists_and_Colorists" title="American Association of Textile Chemists and Colorists">American Association of Textile Chemists and Colorists</a> establish standards for testing of textiles. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:15_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-:15-114">[114]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:16_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-:16-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some examples of tests at different stages:
</p><p>For fiber: Fiber identification is a necessary test for determining fiber content and classifying products. The labelling of items with their fiber content percentage is a regulatory requirement. Using <a href="/wiki/Microscope" title="Microscope">microscopy</a>, solubility, and burn tests, fibers are distinguished from one another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> More fiber relating tests include fiber length, diameter, <a href="/wiki/Cotton_maturity" title="Cotton maturity">Micronaire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>For yarn: <a href="/wiki/Units_of_textile_measurement#Linear_density" title="Units of textile measurement">Yarn count</a>, <a href="/wiki/Units_of_textile_measurement#Denier" title="Units of textile measurement">Denier</a>, Strength, evenness.
</p><p>For fabric: <a href="/wiki/Dimensional_stability_(fabric)" title="Dimensional stability (fabric)">Dimensional stability</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colour_fastness" title="Colour fastness">color fastness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Units_of_textile_measurement#Thread_count" title="Units of textile measurement">thread count</a>, <a href="/wiki/Units_of_textile_measurement#Grams_per_square_metre_(GSM)" title="Units of textile measurement">G.S.M</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pill_(textile)" title="Pill (textile)">pilling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Combustibility_and_flammability" title="Combustibility and flammability">flammability</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:15_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-:15-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:16_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-:16-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Effect of fiber content

''Fiber content is a significant consideration for the design with polymer reinforced composites, as it controls the mechanical, thermomechanical, and tribological performance. Therefore, for particular applications, it is important to identify how the polymer composite behavior reacts with the fiber content under given operating circumstances.''

Fisher is Curator Emirta, Textiles &amp; Costumes of the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_International_Folk_Art" title="Museum of International Folk Art">Museum of International Folk Art</a>.</li>
<li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFGood2006">Good, Irene (2006). "<a class="external text" href="http://digitalcommons.unl.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=1142&amp;context=tsaconf" rel="nofollow">From Kitsch to Art Moderne: Popular Textiles for Women in the First Half of Twentieth-Century Japan</a>" (<a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20150412060754/http://digitalcommons.unl.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=1142&amp;context=tsaconf" rel="nofollow">Archive</a>). <i>Textile Society of America Symposium Proceedings</i>. <a href="/wiki/Textile_Society_of_America" title="Textile Society of America">Textile Society of America</a>, January 1, 1998.</li></ul>
